<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8641d7c8-7227-422b-9982-3078e3dc9dc8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1532744-i-don-t-understand-why-the-collection-is-a">I don&#39;t understand why the collection is a &quot;trilogy&quot; when there are 6 novels originally written by Frank Herbert. I would have thought this would be a &quot;Dune Collection Vol. 1&quot;. What makes the first three unique in the whole of the six?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1532744'><a id="like_count_community_question_1532744" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1532744?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F53764.The_Great_Dune_Trilogy">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1532744-i-don-t-understand-why-the-collection-is-a">one year ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1532744-i-don-t-understand-why-the-collection-is-a">Add your answer</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/2631425-michael"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1538808870i/2631425._UX100_CR0,7,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/2631425-michael">Michael</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The first three books were written relatively close together, are set within a defined time frame, and stood for a long time as a trilogy. After that <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The first three books were written relatively close together, are set within a defined time frame, and stood for a long time as a trilogy. After that publication break, Herbert returned to the series with the next three books, set in the far future of the original trilogy. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_671482617" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/671482617" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/671482617?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer671482617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16459134939049736249">Frank Herbert's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune__Dune_Chronicles__1_' title='Dune (Dune Chronicles #1) by Frank Herbert' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dune</a> was in part inspired by his experience working in a research centre in California studying desertification. The realisation of the interrelationship of environment, people and culture coming out of that experience is a key feature of the series. At the centre of the first novel is a desert planet, Arrakis, and the secret desire of its inhabitants to transform it's ecology. It is a great science-fiction novel about systems of power and the role of ecology, although admittedly</span>
  <span id="freeText16459134939049736249" style="display:none">Frank Herbert's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune__Dune_Chronicles__1_" title="Dune (Dune Chronicles #1) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune</a> was in part inspired by his experience working in a research centre in California studying desertification. The realisation of the interrelationship of environment, people and culture coming out of that experience is a key feature of the series. At the centre of the first novel is a desert planet, Arrakis, and the secret desire of its inhabitants to transform it's ecology. It is a great science-fiction novel about systems of power and the role of ecology, although admittedly delivered in an accept it or loath it writing style and with various weird ideas including: Feudalism in space, a stress on lineages in which nonetheless many of the women seem to be mystic-concubines, homosexuality is shorthand for depraved evil, and space Arabs with blue eyes.<br /><br />The sequels are not fascinating unlike the first novel. Full of enthusiasm after reading <i>Dune</i> I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106.Dune_Messiah__Dune_Chronicles__2_" title="Dune Messiah (Dune Chronicles #2) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune Messiah</a> but it is one of those books that divides the fans from the readers I suppose. <br /><br />In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112.Children_of_Dune__Dune_Chronicles__3_" title="Children of Dune (Dune Chronicles #3) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Children of Dune</a> we see the surface of Arrakis beginning to change as the plans to transform the ecology of the plant are being put into effect, and some of the social implications of those changes beginning to emerge, but the book is not as packed with ideas as Dune. For something similarly ambitious yet more consistent in its delivery (&amp; I lost interest in this series as it ran on and on) I personally prefer Brian Aldiss' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1118348.Helliconia_Trilogy" title="Helliconia Trilogy by Brian W. Aldiss" rel="nofollow noopener">Helliconia Trilogy</a>.<br /><br />The weigh of the ideas is really all placed in the first volume. The Feudal-Federalism of the Space-Empire, the breeding programme to create a Messiah figure who can guide humanity towards an unpredictable future, the land makes the people and the people make the land, the replacement of computers with specialised people. The subsequent books are really just the working through of the ideas set out there. It is all inevitable and the reading as a result is poorer. <br /><br /><i>Dune</i> perhaps epitomises science-fiction. The willingness to embrace big ideas and show them playing out on a broad canvas married to uneven writing and a a certain 'what-the Hell-ness' as the author lays out their pet sociological/anthropological opinions. The David Lynch film, I feel, captures the oddness of the reading experience quite well and perhaps sets about chopping at the text with a brutality which oddly appropriate. Alternatively it offers a combination of the latter books of the Old Testament with a sensitivity towards the influence of the environment upon man and of man upon the environment. At points this works on its own terms, at others it rather strangles itself with its own pretensions. You have to read it to believe it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16459134939049736249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText432993268385871670" style="display:none">The Great Dune Trilogy is an excellent book. I am really enjoyed it. <br /><br />Looking forward to reading the fourth one! 🙂<br /><br />Compare Dune to other science fiction films like Star Wars and Star Trek. I feel Dune is more mature as Dune's storyline is more depth. It feels like that Dune is more an adult version of science fiction than Star Wars and other science fiction films. <br /><br />I also think Dune is unique in that it's quite philosophical. Also the whole story can be used as a political metaphor for imperialism and wars over oil in the middle east. So it's got more depth than most sci fi stories. <br /><br />I also can see a number of ideas taken from Dune for an example, Star Wars and Dune are incredibly similar. In the former, Luke Skywalker hails from a desolate and nearly inhabitable desert planet on the far reaches of the galaxy called Tatooine. In the latter, Paul Atreides and his family move to a desolate and nearly inhabitable desert planet on the far reaches of the known universe called Arrakis. Both sandy planets feature centrally to the plot of their stories; they’re where our respective heroes recieve the call to destiny.<br /><br />Dune 5/5<br />Dune Messiah 5/5<br />Children of Dune 5/5</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5066016103432345654"><br/>This is such a magical book for me. I’m not even going to attempt to write an objective review. I simply don’t have the words.<br/><br/>Yes, this rating is based on emotion and on how this book affected me and my reading evolution over the years. And, frankly, that’s the best way to gauge it, anyway.<br/><br/>Long live the King!<br/><br/><br/><br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2504312778" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2504312778" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/554401-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233528349p2/554401.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2504312778?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/554401-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781473224469" href="/book/show/39329810-the-great-dune-trilogy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2504312778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6364234879180896268">With this latest addition to the so-called Gollancz "Black Library", another classic has been immortalised. If you're looking for a lovely copy to keep, or for a gift, this one has much to like. The black faux-leather has writing imprinted in gold, and it is a nice looking copy. The print type however seems to be of old stock, and a little worn in terms of type - none of that precise digital typography here!<br/><br/>In term of the content, there's nothing too exciting. There are the three books of the or</span>
  <span id="freeText6364234879180896268" style="display:none">With this latest addition to the so-called Gollancz "Black Library", another classic has been immortalised. If you're looking for a lovely copy to keep, or for a gift, this one has much to like. The black faux-leather has writing imprinted in gold, and it is a nice looking copy. The print type however seems to be of old stock, and a little worn in terms of type - none of that precise digital typography here!<br /><br />In term of the content, there's nothing too exciting. There are the three books of the original trilogy, of course (Dune, Dune Messiah and Children of Dune) with the Glossary and the maps as found in the original copies of Dune. There's enough been said in other reviews about the content, so I'll say little here other than Dune is great, the others are more demanding (although, admittedly, not as demanding as the so-called "Second Trilogy", published a decade or so later than these novels.)<br /><br />However, it is the basic text - there's no added introduction or gilt edges (as in the 1987 Easton Press edition of Dune), there's none of the lovely John Schoenherr artwork (recommended by Frank Herbert himself) that was added to the 1999 edition of the Dune Gollancz hardback. It is about half the price of the Folio Press edition of Dune, though, and here you get the other two books as well.<br /><br />But it gives you what you're expecting - a lovely edition of the basic books to keep, and a worthy addition to the Black Library.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6364234879180896268" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17744817971295823299">The mythos the late Mr Herbert Has bequeathed to us either soaring eloquence of vision or just leaves the reader bored. I am of the former. This is mature, intellectual, dramatic science fiction,that still resonates as a warning about power and its dangers. I seem to read this every few years, and never tire, nor fail to gain a new insight. Simply put, glorious.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4018505324622323478">This remains, for the time being, my favourite series. The story is intricate and well developed and perfectly balanced so that you do not lose sight of the other points of view in the book while focusing on a specific character's story. I found this book to be the well of clear water that I needed so badly in a rather dry existence. I remember having read the trilogy as well as the other books in under 2 weeks time, therefore this represents a personal epiphany and these books shall forever be </span>
  <span id="freeText4018505324622323478" style="display:none">This remains, for the time being, my favourite series. The story is intricate and well developed and perfectly balanced so that you do not lose sight of the other points of view in the book while focusing on a specific character's story. I found this book to be the well of clear water that I needed so badly in a rather dry existence. I remember having read the trilogy as well as the other books in under 2 weeks time, therefore this represents a personal epiphany and these books shall forever be the dearest and most valued memories of all that I have read. <br />I highly recommend it for any SF fan (next to Asimov's Foundation).</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amélie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amélie" href="/user/show/4372620-am-lie">Amélie</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer2855154143923937056">(3.5/5)<br/><br/>So. After nearly a month, I am DONE with the Great Dune Trilogy, at last!<br/><br/>One thing to make clear: I'm glad I read it. In terms of scale and cultural legacy, it wouldn't feel wrong to call this SciFi's <i>Lord of the Rings</i>. It's huge, not just in terms of page but when you look at the worldbuilding, the dynasties created, the plot that spans decades. It's also extremely interesting.<br/><br/>My main problem was one of taste: I'm not really into mysticism. As much as I loved the first part of the first </span>
  <span id="freeText2855154143923937056" style="display:none">(3.5/5)<br /><br />So. After nearly a month, I am DONE with the Great Dune Trilogy, at last!<br /><br />One thing to make clear: I'm glad I read it. In terms of scale and cultural legacy, it wouldn't feel wrong to call this SciFi's <i>Lord of the Rings</i>. It's huge, not just in terms of page but when you look at the worldbuilding, the dynasties created, the plot that spans decades. It's also extremely interesting.<br /><br />My main problem was one of taste: I'm not really into mysticism. As much as I loved the first part of the first book, a lot of the rest... I wouldn't say lost me, but just didn't thrill me as much. And even though I can see why this is amazing, it just didn't amaze me personally.<br /><br />Also, even though I do like time skips in general, I found the discrepancies between the two points often jarring, especially between <i>Dune</i> and <i>Dune Messiah</i>. There's so much that happens, so much character development that we don't get to see and struggle to understand. How did Paul get from where he was, psychologically speaking, at the end of <i>Dune</i>, to the start of <i>Dune Messiah</i>? Reading the two back to back, I couldn't really reconcile it. <br /><br />In fact, it may not be so much about time skip as about character development itself. But that would require a lot of spoilers to be discussed in depth, so I'll stop there now.<br /><br />I'm not sure if I'll ever read more about this world. I'm not entirely closing the door on it. I just think it may not be exactly for me, or at least not at this point in time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2855154143923937056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1396515046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1396515046" />
    <a title="Joshua Tintner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4698630-joshua-tintner"><img alt="Joshua Tintner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459824311p2/4698630.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1396515046?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshua Tintner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshua Tintner" href="/user/show/4698630-joshua-tintner">Joshua Tintner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1396515046&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1396515046" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11332964280251788013">My friend warned me: it goes down after the first book. But I couldnt help myself and ordered this trilogy the day after finishing Dune. I was not interested as much in the plot, which can dawdle on forever like so many once-great sci-fi series, but rather in how Frank Herbert continued exploring the big ideas of Dune: the Middle East/Dune parallels, Church-State, climate change, the competing myth creations of Paul vs the BG...<br/><br/>Book II (Dune Messiah) is the fall of Paul and Book III (Children of</span>
  <span id="freeText11332964280251788013" style="display:none">My friend warned me: it goes down after the first book. But I couldnt help myself and ordered this trilogy the day after finishing Dune. I was not interested as much in the plot, which can dawdle on forever like so many once-great sci-fi series, but rather in how Frank Herbert continued exploring the big ideas of Dune: the Middle East/Dune parallels, Church-State, climate change, the competing myth creations of Paul vs the BG...<br /><br />Book II (Dune Messiah) is the fall of Paul and Book III (Children of Dune) focuses on the Atreides who were born on Dune (Paul's sister Alia, and Paul's twins). From a reading of other reviews, many people apparently lost interest at book II; however, I was drawn in by the new characters and intrigued by the big arc of Paul's willing self-destruction. The plot was not as compelling as Dune, but it also was not leaning on the the archetypal outline of the child-turned-hero myth. So as I began book II, I thought that my friend's judgment was off: while the trilogy was not equal parts greatness, it was building upon the masterpiece into something more.<br /><br />Book III got off to a promising start, with new characters (Farad'n, the grandson of the former Shaddah Emperor, and his entourage) and a more abstract focus on the dangers inherent in all of the "children of Dune" as a result of their having been born with a multiplicity of personalities and memories. Leto emerges as the protagonist, who is seeking a path that will correct the errors of his father. <br /><br />At this point, I was still hooked. "What does he see that Paul did not?" "How will this insight shift my understanding of the big issues?" But then, slowly, Leto's thoughts and philosophy became harder for me to follow. Is the Golden Path Leto's rule or Farad'n (who is described with plenty of gold imagery)? Is Leto prescient or has he avoided that temptation? By destroying the qanats, isnt Leto turning the planet back to dessert and thereby saving the worms, but if so, why will the worms die in 100 years? Harum??<br /><br />It was this muddying of the big issues that left me dissatisfied, because if those had stayed coherent in my head (which may be the fault of my reading and not the writing), then I might not have found some of the plot twists so absurd (Leto becoming a worm) or the personality changes unconvincing (Paul to Preacher; Alia to Abomination).<br /><br />So I wont be reading any more Dune books, but I could see myself coming back to this series again and giving the third book another chance. I'd love to find that I had missed subtleties and that Herbert's grand vision was simply too all-encompassing for me to take in at first try.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11332964280251788013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1663833083" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1663833083" />
    <a title="Sebastian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4319355-sebastian"><img alt="Sebastian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596901352p2/4319355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1663833083?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sebastian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sebastian" href="/user/show/4319355-sebastian">Sebastian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780575025868" href="/book/show/2400348.The_Great_Dune_Trilogy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319355-sebastian?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319355-sebastian?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319355-sebastian?shelf=alternate-worlds">alternate-worlds</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319355-sebastian?shelf=scifi-new-wave-1960-1979">scifi-new-wave-1960-1979</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319355-sebastian?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319355-sebastian?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1663833083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14269617432698994686">To the uninitiated the Dune universe and its characters, plots and intrigues might seem inaccessible. And perhaps that is fair comment. The continuously shifting allegiances, uncovering of secrets and see-sawing fulcrum of power can be intimidating. Accompanied by Herbert's rather staid prose, Dune is not recommended as a primer for the novice or the curious, however, those willing to persist will be rewarded many-fold. That is not to say that Dune is difficult to read - it isn't. Dune is the re</span>
  <span id="freeText14269617432698994686" style="display:none">To the uninitiated the Dune universe and its characters, plots and intrigues might seem inaccessible. And perhaps that is fair comment. The continuously shifting allegiances, uncovering of secrets and see-sawing fulcrum of power can be intimidating. Accompanied by Herbert's rather staid prose, Dune is not recommended as a primer for the novice or the curious, however, those willing to persist will be rewarded many-fold. That is not to say that Dune is difficult to read - it isn't. Dune is the reason why Sci-fi exists. It is the most magnificent theatre of the fantastic, the grandiose and the epic, whilst still retaining it's integrity.<br /><br />Sci-fi is also a potent vehicle for social commentary. Not bound by the constraints of other forms of fiction, Sci-fi has a virtual blank canvas upon which to paint its social allegory, allowing it unprecedented scope and accuracy. Frank Herbert makes full use of these faculties and delivers a typically honest interrogation of both temporal and existential issues such as imperialism, globalisation (universalisation?), organised religion, free will vs predestination, and fundamentalism, amongst others. Not only that but Herbert pioneered planetary ecology in creating an ecosystem for Arrakis, an ecosystem that undergoes some drastic transformations during the course of the book, with remarkable accuracy have reported many ecologists.<br /><br />But most incredibly of all, Herbert somehow created an allegory that pre-dated its subject by almost three decades, an allegory that is still as pertinent now as it was 20 years ago. Without giving too much away, the world of Dune (trilogy) and everything that takes place in it can be viewed as a direct analogy for Western, specifically US, involvement in the Middle East from Gulf War I hence, with particular reference to the conquest for oil (melange/spice). The Fremen culture is closely modelled on Arab culture, even speaking practically pure Arabic.<br /><br />But what freaks me out, and many Arab readers, is that some of the Arabic words and concepts employed by Herbert in Dune did not become part of mainstream Arabic culture until the 21st century (the first book was published in 1965)! <br /><br />Obviously I am a big fan - Dune was the book that eventually tilted me towards the Sci-fi windmill - but I believe this book deserves recognition as a great work of fiction, not just Science Fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14269617432698994686" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2114736314" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6259449535404436560">After hearing so many times that Dune was the masterpiece of Science Fiction literature, and seeing a new edition in the store where before I had seen none, I decided to pick this up. This took me some months to read, specially because it is very dense and in other editions just the first book is 900-ish pages. But it was absolutely worth it, entrancing and mind-blowing! Herbert succeeds at juxtaposing so many different elements: ecology, religion, consciousness, feudalism, space travel... gawsh</span>
  <span id="freeText6259449535404436560" style="display:none">After hearing so many times that Dune was the masterpiece of Science Fiction literature, and seeing a new edition in the store where before I had seen none, I decided to pick this up. This took me some months to read, specially because it is very dense and in other editions just the first book is 900-ish pages. But it was absolutely worth it, entrancing and mind-blowing! Herbert succeeds at juxtaposing so many different elements: ecology, religion, consciousness, feudalism, space travel... gawsh, I could go on! He delivers lessons on all of these, and I couldn't help but save so many quotes. The fact that he has as the epicentre of the story an entheogen is... just amazing. Hardly anyone writes about that because they either have no experience in psychoactive substances, or they are too afraid of the negative connotations it might carry. But not Herbert. He would be the kind of person I'd love to have a chat with. The fabric he's woven with these books is just incredible.<br /> <br />It is a mainly character-driven plot, with the three books in this trilogy focusing on the Atreides family on Dune. Conversations leave you in suspense because there are so many hidden motivations, giving you an exaggerated perspective on human nature. That being said, I sometimes had a hard time to follow Herbert's writing and dialogues. I suspect that is partly why this trilogy took me so long to complete, I need to be really focused and in the mood to dive into such a complex world.<br />Also, my order of preference is: (1) Dune; (2) Dune Messiah and (3) Children of Dune. There were some plot decisions in the last book that just left me baffled, and I was wondering why the characters would decide to act that way. Dune just had a hold on me from start to finish, and I was finding it hard to put it down, already thinking of the fun I would have in rereading it. Dune Messiah just focused so much more on Paul's struggles in his new found role, I felt much closer to him, and the whole book just had a poetic justice undertone that I really liked.<br /><br />Even though there were some low points, I can't help giving this trilogy a 5* rating! Simply because I think everyone should delve into this world, let themselves be absorbed by it. It makes you question your reality, your choices, your relationships and the impact you have on the world, or the impact the world has on you. I cannot recommend it enough, and believe me, I can't shut up about it. I will definitely continue with the the sequels!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6259449535404436560" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2675262951" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2675262951" />
    <a title="Lee Penney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7402485-lee-penney"><img alt="Lee Penney" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2675262951?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Penney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Penney" href="/user/show/7402485-lee-penney">Lee Penney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2675262951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3397561618419345997">Breaking down each book:<br/><br/>Dune - 4*<br/><br/>I was aware of Dune from the movie, which is one of my favourites (it’s well worth a watch, because the design team was clearly on something strong). I knew it was based on a book and I’d been meaning to read it for a while.<br/><br/>I actually have the first three books in an omnibus edition.<br/><br/>One thing that struck me is how closely the movie sticks to the plot of the novel, lifting lines and entire scenes directly from the book — not something that happens very often.<br/><br/>Where</span>
  <span id="freeText3397561618419345997" style="display:none">Breaking down each book:<br /><br />Dune - 4*<br /><br />I was aware of Dune from the movie, which is one of my favourites (it’s well worth a watch, because the design team was clearly on something strong). I knew it was based on a book and I’d been meaning to read it for a while.<br /><br />I actually have the first three books in an omnibus edition.<br /><br />One thing that struck me is how closely the movie sticks to the plot of the novel, lifting lines and entire scenes directly from the book — not something that happens very often.<br /><br />Where it differs, I’d actually say the movie does a better job. Controversial, I know.<br /><br />I say that because, while the book provides a bit more depth to certain elements, it seems shy of the action. Herbert loves inventing new terms, sects and religious elements, but any action passes in a few paragraphs or happens entirely off-screen.<br /><br />Other than that, it’s brilliantly inventive, with scenes that I couldn’t pull myself away from sprinkled throughout.<br /><br />I did find it hard to read without comparing it to the movie. The next next two should give me a different view.<br /><br />They’re apparently re-making the movie for release in 2019, but having read it, I think it would have made a good TV series with as much murder and double-dealing as Game of Thrones.<br /><br />---<br /><br />Dune Messiah 3*<br /><br />This is a short entry compared to the others in the trilogy. It also involves a lot more navel-gazing than the first.<br /><br />Gone are the battles, the fighting and the grand visions.<br /><br />They’re replaced with plots, intrigue and battles of conscience. Having created an empire, set a galaxy-wide jihad in motion and been deified by his people, the new emperor must deal with his own inner demons.<br /><br />There are no easy choices, but it seems to be less about the plots from without and more about the roads he must walk and the decisions he has to live with.<br /><br />As such, it’s a deep read, but lacks something in the dynamism.<br /><br />Pleasant enough but doesn’t have the grandeur of the first.<br /><br />---<br /><br />Children of Dune 3*<br /><br />I picked up the miniseries that stars James McAvoy on DVD a while back and was suitably lost in the story, and the low production value. I was hoping this would help make sense of it.<br /><br />This was the final part of the three-book collection I had.<br /><br />As the name suggests, it follows the children of Paul Maud’dib as they grow into adolescence on Dune, raised away from the cities as Fremen in a sietch (cave complex).<br /><br />There’s some very good parts, some interesting action, some gripping moments as the pair, who look like children but have the memories of all of their ancestors, dish out philosophy and wisdom. Not all of it made sense though.<br /><br />And then there’s the ending. It’s a mess. Aside from being unbelievably abrupt, it just doesn’t fit with the rest of the story.<br /><br />The narrative, up to that point, is more convoluted and nuanced than anything in Game of Thrones, annoyingly so at times, but the ending feels like the author was trying to get it under a certain word count and realised he was running out.<br /><br />Maybe a second read would help, but not likely I’ll give it that chance.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3397561618419345997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3606635982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3606635982" />
    <a title="Megan Lowde" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/102464080-megan-lowde"><img alt="Megan Lowde" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579893957p2/102464080.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3606635982?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Lowde" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Lowde" href="/user/show/102464080-megan-lowde">Megan Lowde</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781473224469" href="/book/show/39329810-the-great-dune-trilogy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/102464080-megan-lowde?shelf=personal-library">personal-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3606635982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8431587347135806150"><i>
  <b>Dune</b>
</i><br/>3/5 stars - This was an interesting read, and difficult at first. It really threw you in at the deep end with a whole load of unfamiliar terms, characters, worlds and political set-ups, but once I got more into it and understood what was happening I couldn't help but become absorbed with the story. There was something so fascinating about Arrakis in particular, with the sandworms and the Fremen and the secrets of the spice and the water. The characters as well I became more invested in a</span>
  <span id="freeText8431587347135806150" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>Dune</b>
</i><br />3/5 stars - This was an interesting read, and difficult at first. It really threw you in at the deep end with a whole load of unfamiliar terms, characters, worlds and political set-ups, but once I got more into it and understood what was happening I couldn't help but become absorbed with the story. There was something so fascinating about Arrakis in particular, with the sandworms and the Fremen and the secrets of the spice and the water. The characters as well I became more invested in as the story progressed, though Paul - who I had a certain fondness for at first - becomes increasingly more unlikeable as the story progresses, as does Jessica, much to my disappointment. <br />The story itself spans a large expanse of time - covering several years - and in it we see a great political change with the rise of Paul as the Muad'Dib, which was implied all along through the inclusion of excerpts from pieces written about him by the Princess Irulan - <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[who is now to be engaged to, which will prove interesting as she writes extensively about him <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br />I did have my issues with the story as a whole - one of those being that there is a whole lot of talking, and a whole lot of pages, but there still seems to hardly be any <i>action</i> and I could probably tick off the decisive plot points on one hand - but I will be interested to see how it continues, and I can see why it is considered to be a classic of science fiction. I did also enjoy it more than I thought I was, especially upon my reading of the first 50 pages where I struggled immensely to get into it, but still there was nothing about the story I truly loved. <br /><i>
  <b>Dune Messiah</b>
</i><br />2/5 stars - There were so many plots within plots in this one that I found it difficult to keep up with all of the political manoeuvrings, and the story just wasn't quite as compelling as I had found it to be in the original <i>Dune</i>. I spent so much of my time reading this trying to reconcile the ending of <i>Dune</i> with the reality twelve years later, and I didn't really like what I saw, though the ending at least came as a little surprise with the twins and Paul's leaving for the desert, alongside the return of <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Duncan Idaho, and his romance with Alia <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br />In all, it was a far shorter novel than <i>Dune</i> and didn't seem to add all that much to the series besides denoting Paul's spiralling deification and the jihad that arose out of his name. Still, I will be interested to see the end of this series, if only to see what becomes of his children. <br /><i>
  <b>Children of Dune</b>
</i><br />2/5 stars - Much like <i>Dune Messiah</i>, I really couldn't get into this one as much. Here we have the descent of Alia into madness as she is possessed by the various personalities inside of her, most notably the Baron. We also follow the twins, Leto and Ghanima, and of course Jessica is still kicking around. It just seemed to all drag on a little bit for me and it's carrying on the same old political machinations and assassinations just with different characters. Hopefully the rest of the series will offer something a little different. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8431587347135806150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2427478637" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2427478637" />
    <a title="Ian King" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36611593-ian-king"><img alt="Ian King" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442988295p2/36611593.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2427478637?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian King" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian King" href="/user/show/36611593-ian-king">Ian King</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2427478637" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3252280816358390443">Wow! What an adventure! This is a brilliant read and a well laid out story, set on the desert planet 'Dune'. A place where the universe is battling to control the 'Spice' found on this wretched land. This is a story of corruption, control and universal domination between various races who will betray one another at the drop of the hat. Whoever pays the highest price get's the greatest army, or so they think.<br/>Baron Harkonnen thinks he has the new planet administrator, 'The Duke of Atreides', wrapp</span>
  <span id="freeText3252280816358390443" style="display:none">Wow! What an adventure! This is a brilliant read and a well laid out story, set on the desert planet 'Dune'. A place where the universe is battling to control the 'Spice' found on this wretched land. This is a story of corruption, control and universal domination between various races who will betray one another at the drop of the hat. Whoever pays the highest price get's the greatest army, or so they think.<br />Baron Harkonnen thinks he has the new planet administrator, 'The Duke of Atreides', wrapped around his fat finger and so he does, but the Baron had not accounted for The Dukes prophesied son <br />Paul. Paul is a long awaited production of the 'Bene-Jeseret' experiment, which has been in development for many generations. A Bene-Jeseret is born and raised through a selection process of the highest order and of the best training known. Once Paul Atreides comes of age, he is put through the terrible 'Jongebar' test which could cost him his life and he survives with unprecedented results. <br />A brutal plot is afoot to take absolute control of 'The Spice' and The Atreides family line is destroyed, only Paul and his mother survive and go into hiding and become allied with the planets local natives. They will rise again, a most powerful army that sets the universe on its edge.<br />It's a long trilogy and sometimes hard to follow, but certainly worth your time.<br />I listened to the audiobook version of this (over 22 hours) and was a little disappointed with this, as the characters voices and narration changed constantly. This is the only reason I've given it a 4 star. I found that the main narrator sometimes played the individual characters, which is fine, but other times there were other people doing those same characters so there was no real consistency to the audio flow. I found this distracting because I was expecting a particular character voice to be heard and it would have been even more enjoyable if the production company got this one right. They should have stuck to either the single narrator, or the different character voices playing their parts throughout... one, or the other but definitely not both. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3252280816358390443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vCC2e15WCL9pMm31jyiWNs8ArDbr0oBYdrRJA2KH/EU26gzifwJ1vuu12NO2EnlDA5Lj4dBbiGiLEl19c8YNVw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('83p1riMDFHvYOiLnyh4s0rKdMevJ2nT2eepNyUW/3yF5sM83Aldpelq9l8HzJMOnfg9+PPJTfMaETFm3VP4uMw==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer14096437378145477788">Frank Herbert was a critically acclaimed and commercially successful American science fiction author.<br/><br/>He is best known for the novel <i>Dune</i> and its five sequels. The Dune saga, set in the distant future and taking place over millennia, dealt with themes such as human survival and evolution, ecology, and the intersection of religion, politics, and power, and is widely considered to be among the classi</span>
  <span id="freeText14096437378145477788" style="display:none">Frank Herbert was a critically acclaimed and commercially successful American science fiction author.<br /><br />He is best known for the novel <i>Dune</i> and its five sequels. The Dune saga, set in the distant future and taking place over millennia, dealt with themes such as human survival and evolution, ecology, and the intersection of religion, politics, and power, and is widely considered to be among the classics in the field of science fiction.<br /><br />He was the father of fellow author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/56.Brian_Herbert" title="Brian Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Brian Herbert</a>.</span>

            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
